17/08/2023, 20:37 - .: fn main() {
    let mut num1: i32 = 10;
    let mut num2: i32 = 20;

    println!("Numbers before calculation: \nnum1:{}\nnum2:{}", num1, num2);

    // Addition
    let sum = add_numbers(&mut num1, &mut num2);
    println!("The sum is: {}", sum);

    // Subtraction
    let difference = subtract_numbers(&mut num1, &mut num2);
    println!("The difference is: {}", difference);

    // Multiplication
    let product = multiply_numbers(&mut num1, &mut num2);
    println!("The product is: {}", product);

    // Division
    let quotient = divide_numbers(&mut num1, &mut num2);
    println!("The quotient is: {}", quotient);
}

fn add_numbers(num1: &mut i32, num2: &mut i32) -> i32 {
    *num1 += *num2;
    return *num1;
}

fn subtract_numbers(num1: &mut i32, num2: &mut i32) -> i32 {
    *num1 -= *num2;
    return *num1;
}

fn multiply_numbers(num1: &mut i32, num2: &mut i32) -> i32 {
    *num1 *= *num2;
    return *num1;
}

fn divide_numbers(num1: &mut i32, num2: &mut i32) -> i32 {
    *num1 /= *num2;
    return *num1;
}

23/08/2023, 20:59 - Chhoti: Here are the answers to your questions:

1. **What is the primary benefit of using generics in Rust?**
   - **Correct Answer:** Greater code flexibility

2. **Which of the following is a valid Rust function definition using generics?**
   - **Correct Answer:** fn add<T>(x: T, y: T) -> T { x + y }

3. **In Rust, what is the syntax for declaring a generic type parameter?**
   - **Correct Answer:** <T>

4. **Which of the following statements is true about generic types in Rust?**
   - **Correct Answer:** Generic types are specified at compile time

5. **Which of the following is not a benefit of using generics in Rust?**
   - **Correct Answer:** Improved runtime performance

6. **Which of the following is not a valid Rust trait bound syntax?**
   - **Correct Answer:** where T: (Trait + OtherTrait)

7. **What is the purpose of a trait bound in Rust?**
   - **Correct Answer:** To specify the allowed types for a generic type parameter

8. **Which of the following is a valid Rust syntax for specifying multiple generic type parameters?**
   - **Correct Answer:** fn my_func<T1, T2>(x: T1, y: T2) -> T1 { x }

9. **In Rust, what is the difference between using a generic type parameter and a concrete type as a function argument?**
   - **Correct Answer:** Using a generic type parameter allows the function to work with multiple types, while a concrete type restricts the function to only work with that type

10. **What is the purpose of the `where` keyword when used with a generic type parameter in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** To specify the constraints on the type parameter

11. **What is a trait in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** A set of methods that types can implement

12. **Which of the following is a valid syntax for defining a trait in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** trait MyTrait { }

13. **In Rust, can a struct implement multiple traits?**
    - **Correct Answer:** Yes

14. **What is the syntax for implementing a trait for a struct in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** impl MyTrait for MyStruct { }

15. **Which of the following is not a benefit of using traits in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** Improved runtime performance

16. **In Rust, what is the purpose of a default method implementation in a trait?**
    - **Correct Answer:** To provide a fallback implementation for types that don't implement the method

17. **In Rust, can a trait be implemented for types outside of the current crate?**
    - **Correct Answer:** Yes

18. **What is the syntax for calling a trait method on a generic type parameter in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** x.my_trait_method()

19. **Which of the following is true about trait objects in Rust?**
    - **Correct Answer:** They can be used to store any type that implements the trait

20. **In Rust, can a trait method have default parameter values?**
    - **Correct Answer:** No

23/08/2023, 21:21 - Chhoti: use std::collections::HashMap;

// Define the SortByKey trait
trait SortByKey<K, V> {
    fn sort_by_key(&mut self);
}

// Implement the SortByKey trait for the HashMap struct
impl<K: Ord, V> SortByKey<K, V> for HashMap<K, V> {
    fn sort_by_key(&mut self) {
        // Create a sorted vector of key-value pairs
        let mut sorted_pairs: Vec<(K, V)> = self.drain().collect();
        sorted_pairs.sort_by_key(|(key, _)| key.clone());

        // Insert the sorted pairs back into the HashMap
        for (key, value) in sorted_pairs {
            self.insert(key, value);
        }
    }
}

fn main() {
    // Create a new instance of the generic HashMap struct
    let mut my_map: HashMap<i32, String> = HashMap::new();

    // Add key-value pairs to the map
    my_map.insert(3, "Three".to_string());
    my_map.insert(1, "One".to_string());
    my_map.insert(2, "Two".to_string());

    println!("Original map: {:?}", my_map);

    // Sort the elements in the map by their keys
    my_map.sort_by_key();

    println!("Sorted map: {:?}", my_map);
}

We import the HashMap struct from the standard library.
We define the SortByKey trait with a single method sort_by_key, which will sort the elements in the map by their key values.
We implement the SortByKey trait for the HashMap struct. Inside the implementation, we create a sorted vector of key-value pairs, sort it based on the keys, and then insert the sorted pairs back into the map.
In the main function, we create a new HashMap instance and add some key-value pairs to it.
We print the original map's contents and then call the sort_by_key method to sort the map by keys.
Finally, we print the sorted map's contents.

24/08/2023, 20:18 - Chhoti: use std::collections::HashMap;

// Define the SortByKey trait
trait SortByKey<K, V> {
    fn sort_by_key(&mut self);
}

// Implement the SortByKey trait for the HashMap struct
impl<K: Ord, V> SortByKey<K, V> for HashMap<K, V> {
    fn sort_by_key(&mut self) {
        let mut sorted_pairs: Vec<_> = self.drain().collect();
        sorted_pairs.sort_by(|(key1, _), (key2, _)| key1.cmp(key2));
        for (key, value) in sorted_pairs {
            self.insert(key, value);
        }
    }
}

fn main() {
    // Create a new instance of HashMap
    let mut my_map: HashMap<i32, String> = HashMap::new();

    // Add key-value pairs
    my_map.insert(3, "Three".to_string());
    my_map.insert(1, "One".to_string());
    my_map.insert(4, "Four".to_string());
    my_map.insert(2, "Two".to_string());

    println!("Original map: {:?}", my_map);

    // Sort the map by keys
    my_map.sort_by_key();

    println!("Sorted map: {:?}", my_map);
}
